Summary of Key Trends Analysis in Manufacturing AI Transformation (AX)

According to the latest analysis results, the adoption of AI in the manufacturing industry is extending beyond simple process optimization to business model innovation. The main trends are as follows.

First, Advancement of Intelligent Factory Operations. Predictive maintenance, AI vision inspection, and real-time production monitoring have become core technologies that maximize productivity.

Second, Expansion of Digital Twin-based Virtualization. The entire process from product development to production is simulated in a virtual environment, reducing costs and time, and minimizing risks.

Third, Emergence of New Paradigms such as Generative AI and Circular Manufacturing. Generative AI is driving innovation in areas like design automation, and circular manufacturing, combined with Digital Product Passports (DPP), is becoming a new strategic objective for creating sustainable value.

To successfully seize these opportunities, securing high-quality data and establishing a governance system, and acquiring MLOps (Model Operations and Management) capabilities to stably operate AI models are key challenges. A strategic approach based on clear business objectives is necessary.

IBM presented an approximately $300 billion opportunity in manufacturing with AI, along with the potential for 2-3% improvement in productivity/profitability. Based on the fact that 65% of factories utilize connected equipment data and 35% have adopted autonomous operations, companies should 1) select priority tasks linked to clear business metrics (e.g., OEE, prediction accuracy), 2) start with areas that yield immediate significant effects, such as predictive maintenance and vision inspection, 3) standardize data/model governance and MLOps, centered around CAIO, 4) leverage CSP's 5G/edge infrastructure to enhance real-time quality/equipment utilization, and 5) extend AI beyond individual process units to the entire supply chain to establish closed-loop operations, which is key. This prevents the proliferation of disconnected pilot projects and provides a practical roadmap for systematically raising productivity, profitability, and prediction accuracy.

CarbonSix's Sigma Kit standardizes/bundles imitation learning-based robots into algorithms, grippers, sensors, and teaching tools, reducing SI dependency and integration risk, and enabling quick application to variable and unstructured processes even without specialized personnel. Initial pilots are suitable for pick-and-place variations, kitting, and simple assembly/inspection; track teaching time, change response lead time, cycle time variations, first pass yield, and operator acceptance as KPIs, and simultaneously manage safety certification/safety I-O, MES/PLC integration, data quality, and vendor dependency/maintenance risk.

CarbonSix's Sigma Kit is a standard robot kit for manufacturing that utilizes imitation learning, designed to quickly teach and deploy robots without additional equipment or highly skilled personnel. By providing algorithms, grippers, sensors, and teaching tools as an integrated package, it reduces integration complexity and increases adaptability to highly variable tasks. This has the potential to enhance the economic viability of automation in high-mix low-volume production and unstructured processes, and during piloting, an approach of verifying its effectiveness with KPIs such as teaching time, changeover lead time, cycle time variation, and first pass yield is effective.

By coordinating and integrating automation, AI, IoT, and blockchain, E2E traceability and closed-loop resource recovery can be implemented across the entire supply chain, thereby achieving both digital transformation and environmental sustainability. This research abstract presents strategic directions for implementing circular manufacturing by integrating automation, AI, IoT, and blockchain. In particular, it emphasizes a framework where material and component lineage collected via IoT is reliably shared through blockchain, AI optimizes remaining useful life, disassembly/sorting, and quality grading, and automation increases the efficiency of recovery and remanufacturing processes. Furthermore, by clearly addressing challenges such as data interoperability, governance, energy efficiency, and organizational readiness, it proposes actionable points to practitioners like standard adoption, access management, energy-efficient design, and change management. Although case studies and quantitative metrics are lacking, it provides valuable strategic insights for technology combinations and roadmap conceptualization to scale up the circular economy.

This review presents immediately applicable levers through AI in manufacturing AX, such as equipment and line energy optimization (NILM/LSTM), automation of early design LCA prediction, AGV routing and vision-based waste classification within factories, and Blockchain+AI carbon traceability. A review synthesizing 62 papers on AI-environmental sustainability research from 2018-2024. Key insights directly applicable to manufacturing include: 1) Equipment and line energy optimization using LSTM, NILM, etc., 2) Accelerating eco-friendly design decisions with NLP/ML-based LCA prediction, 3) In-factory application of AGV routing optimization based on port case studies, 4) Vision-based waste classification and WtE process advancement, 5) AI achieving >5% energy efficiency in many industries and >20% emissions reduction in some projects, and 6) Enhancing supply chain carbon transparency with AI+blockchain. Simultaneously, it highlights 'carbon lock-in' risks due to data centers and IoT, and variations in effectiveness across regions and industries. Manufacturers should prioritize implementing energy management pilots (NILM/prediction), LCA-ML tools, AGV optimization PoCs, vision-based classification, establishing carbon traceability, model lightweighting and edge deployment, and integrating with Green HRM.

The semiconductor industry's workforce and CAPEX adjustments and portfolio streamlining are 'selective investment' and core competency concentration strategies that manufacturing companies should adopt during economic fluctuations, presenting practical measures to reduce supply chain risks by prioritizing automation ROI (predictive maintenance, OEE, energy), strengthening S&OP, and modular standardization. In semiconductor manufacturing, as AI demand and adoption simultaneously expand, process optimization and yield improvement are key challenges. On-site priorities include the demonstration and diffusion of APC/VM and defect analysis AI, MESâ€“APCâ€“EDA data standardization, and real-time SPC establishment. In response to the growth of advanced packaging/testing and heterogeneous integration, joint development among foundries, OSATs, and equipment manufacturers, along with the promotion of packaging line digital twin / thermal, electrical, and mechanical co-simulation, are being pursued. Concurrently, focus on 14â€“28nm legacy automation and OEE improvement, as well as the precision of 5nm and below EUV/high-NA metrology and contamination control. Additionally, in line with the expansion of SiC/GaN, strengthen NDT-linked inspection/classification AI and high-voltage reliability automation. Furthermore, an integrated AX roadmap is needed, reflecting localization risks, incorporating multi-fab digital twin, multi-sourcing, energy and chemical optimization, and MLOps, DoE, and SPC upskilling.

Due to the soaring demand for AI, FC-BGA has emerged as a packaging bottleneck, and high growth is expected until 2030. Therefore, Korean companies must transition to a server, AI, and automotive portfolio centered on proactive CAPEX and high-layer count/fine-pitch wiring. From a manufacturing AX perspective, the key is to improve yield and lead time by introducing 'Dream Factory'-type advanced automation, SPC/APC, PdM, and Digital Twins, and to quantify ROI using KPIs such as L/S limits, yield, OEE, and COQ. Simultaneously, supply chain bottlenecks must be proactively managed through LTA, JDP, and quality certification targeting ecosystems like SK Hynix-NVIDIA, as well as multi-sourcing, long-term contracts, and safety stock for ABF, copper foil, and fine processing equipment.

With the expansion of AI demand, FC-BGA substrates are emerging as both a packaging bottleneck and a growth point, and Samsung Electro-Mechanics and LG Innotek are aiming for yield and productivity advantages through large-scale capacity expansion and advanced automation. We present quantitative results indicating that if generative AI elevates marketing and customer management productivity by 10%, the GDP of all industries could increase by approximately 5.6% within 10 years, and manufacturing's production and investment demand could expand in tandem. Manufacturers should integrate AI-based demand signals such as customer equity and CAC/LTV into S&OP, monitor the AI adoption rate of downstream customers (e.g., distribution and services) as a leading indicator, and prepare for surging demand by proactively securing bottleneck equipment and key components and implementing phased CAPEX. Simultaneously, internalize commercial tools such as Configure, Price, Quote (CPQ), customer support chatbots, and Account-Based Marketing (ABM) to convert indirect demand spillover into proprietary sales, and re-examine conditions like price indexation and long-term contracts to manage margin risks.

This paper quantitatively demonstrates that the productivity improvement of generative AI, by reducing 'marketing/customer management friction,' causes demand spillover across all industries and the movement of labor and capital to manufacturing (GDP +5.6% within 10 years). Manufacturing needs to include customer capital indicators in S&OP, monitor the AI adoption speed of downstream (distribution, IT, professional services) customers as a leading indicator, and develop CAPEX/supply plans to pre-secure bottleneck equipment and key components in anticipation of a surge in demand. Furthermore, it is advantageous to convert demand expansion into company revenue through the application of generative AI at customer touchpoints such as CPQ, customer support automation, and ABM, and to pre-design pricing/contract structures and workforce redeployment strategies.

This article addresses the emergence of quantum AI as an alternative to compensate for the limitations of GPU-based AI, and presents a specific case from the pharmaceutical sector where 1.1 million data points were processed with 16 qubits to derive new drug candidates. The bottleneck for AI diffusion in manufacturing sites is not algorithms, but power, cooling, compute, and semiconductor supply chains. Therefore, calculating power/network capacity for each factory and designing an edge-HPC/cloud hybrid architecture must be prioritized. Secure operational rates through microgrids, on-site generation (solar + battery/CHP), and demand response and peak-shift based nighttime learning scheduling. For AI servers and accelerators, reduce lead-time risks by expanding domestic/near-shore procurement, dual-sourcing BoM (Bill of Materials), and verifying alternative processes. Additionally, leverage government-private support such as CHIPS/defense programs with a standardized COTS (Commercial Off-The-Shelf) stack to shorten investment and construction periods, and connect these to smart factory initiatives such as predictive maintenance, internal logistics optimization, and autonomous transport.

This article emphasizes that investment in power grids and semiconductor manufacturing infrastructure is key to securing US AI competitiveness. From a manufacturing perspective, it suggests that the core risk of AI adoption lies in securing power and compute resources, and that utilization rate and costs can be optimized through a hybrid design of in-factory edge/HPC and cloud, off-peak training scheduling, and energy strategies like microgrids and DR (Demand Response). Furthermore, an effective approach involves reducing AI server lead-time risks through domestic/near-sourcing and dual-sourcing strategies for semiconductor and accelerator procurement, and shortening deployment time through CAPEX (Capital Expenditure) mitigation utilizing government programs and the adoption of COTS (Commercial Off-The-Shelf) technology. Large-scale generative AI sees a rapid increase in electricity and water consumption (electricity can increase 24-fold, 5 drops of water and energy equivalent to 9 seconds of TV per prompt), which directly impacts TCO, ESG, power capacity, and operational policies in manufacturing AX.
